        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    </tr>
    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>Surprising as it may be to those that have watched him play, Prado is on thinner ice than it may first appear. For one, he's a negative defender at his primary position (-12.4 UZR/150 career at second base). Another surprising discovery is that his slugging percentage (.462), though believable for a semi-powerful middle infielder, is significantly better than his best minor league season (.422 SLG in 2004, as a 20-year-old in Sally League ball). Maybe he's just showing that he can handle Major League fastballs (+15.1 runs career), and if that’s true he can probably continue to put up an OPS over .800. Or maybe that high BABIP (.339) of his won't be sustainable if he doesn't pick up his speed (speed score down to 2.0 from 5.5 in ‘08).<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>With a nod to his secondary statistics, it still seems that Prado has a knack for Major League fastballs. And as long as he can hit better than Omar Infante, he should own the starting second base job in Atlanta. General Manager Frank Wren recently confirmed that Prado will go into the season as the starter, so the worst-case scenario seems to be that he is a capable middle infielder that can hit for an empty batting average. In some ways you might describe his 2009 the same way for fantasy purposes. However, if your league counts OPS, it's worth knowing that his career minor league OPS was .747 and he may be due for a decline in that category. Some of those doubles and triples might be turning into singles, especially if Prado continues to show mediocre wheels with his speed on the base paths. (Eno Sarris)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Prado wore down at the end of the season, slumping noticeably before finally suffering a season-ending oblique injury. But no sooner had he established himself as one of the premier second basemen in the league than the Braves traded for Dan Uggla and shifted Prado to left field, where his bat looks a bit more ordinary. Still, Prado has had a pretty established level of performance at this point: his 2009 and 2010 are almost identical in their triple-slash lines, and with Prado established as the Braves’ leadoff hitter, he’s likely to score a lot more runs. He doesn’t run much, but he’s pretty good bet for another .300 average and homers in the teens, which plays anywhere on the diamond -- last year, Prado was one of only 19 players in the Majors to hit .300 with more than 12 homers. He was an All-Star, but he still could be underrated, especially because of his unusual 2B/LF eligibility. (Alex Remington)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>He's a left fielder now, but Prado's a good bet to bat .300 no matter where he plays.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>One of the unluckiest hitters last season, Martin Prado had a 49 point difference between his batting average on balls in play and the expected version of that stat, which was the 21st-highest unluckiest mark among players with at least 400 plate appearances. If he had hit close to his .315 xBABIP, he probably would have hit .300 for the fourth straight season. It’s something that Prado needs to do to stay relevant in most formats, as he isn't elite in any other category. One thing that did change last season was that Prado hit far fewer line drives than he had in the past, which is worrisome, since batting average on line drives is usually fairly high. But since Prado’s swing percentages didn’t change, perhaps it is a one-year blip. He will have third base and outfield eligibility in most formats, and while he doesn’t hit for enough power to be a viable outfielder starter, he should be just fine at third base, especially if he gets his average back up. (Paul Swydan)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Prado had a down year, but if his batting average on balls in play regresses to previously established levels, he should once again be a good sleeper.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Prado had a very nice bounce-back year after hitting just .260 in 2011, and now will get an opportunity to hit in one of the best hitting ballparks in the league as he moves to Arizona. This past year, he added speed to his arsenal which made him even more fantasy relevant, while also adding positional eligibility at shortstop and second base for most formats. Going forward, if he is able to add mid-teens home runs with mid-teens steals, along with his regular .290-.310 batting average, he can be a very productive and useful fantasy player. The fact that he can be used in multiple positions and plays every day enhances his value a ton as well. His walk rate increasing to a career high of 8.4% (min. 50 plate appearances) is a solid quality as well and if that trend continues he could see more opportunities to steal and score runs. (Ben Duronio)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Prado is a very useful fantasy player due to his multi-position eligibility and ability to help a team in almost every facet. He is not great specifically at any one category, though his batting average is usually well above the league mean, but he can help in four categories and will likely bat first or second in a solid offense. Prado is expected to play third base this year in Arizona, so his multi-position eligibility could expire after this season.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>We have a pretty good grip on who Martin Prado is at this point. Despite some fluctuations in batting average on balls in play and a speed outbreak in 2012, there aren't many factors that make projecting Prado to difficult. He has two straight years with 150 R+RBI, five straight years with between 10 and 15 home runs, and he has only stolen more than five bases once in a season. That's four of the roto categories that can be projected with relative ease. The fifth, batting average, has gone up and down because of an inconsistent, but consistently variable, BABIP. It was slightly down last year and way down in 2011 (although a career low line drive rate drove that). And it was above average in 2012, 2010 and 2009. For his career, he has a .311 BABIP and a .293 average. That's what should be expected, but there's no guarantee you'll get it. The nice thing about Prado's projection is that it's unlikely that any of his roto numbers decrease significantly, while his batting average has the potential to improve. If it does, he'll be a marginal value relative to his draft day price. He's an excellent guy to fill your corner infield, middle infield, or fourth outfielder spot. You can often address positional needs with those players, but if your team looks fairly balanced when it's Prado's time to be selected, he's a reliable option. (<a href="https://twitter.com/TheRealTAL">Brett Talley</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Prado is easily projectable and thus a solid option for your middle infield, corner infield and fourth/fifth outfielder spots. His average is really his only roto stat that has seen a lot of variance lately (with the exception of a speed outlier in 2012). And given that his average was down a bit last year, he could be a bit of a value with a rebound. If not, he likely wouldn't return much less value than what it cost to acquire him.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Prado re-invigorated himself after the Diamondbacks traded him to the Yankees last season. Before you decide to move him up the draft board due to Yankee Stadium, recall that he was then swapped to Miami -- the place where power dies. What you'll probably get from Prado as a fantasy owner is a decent average, versatility, and some runs scored (assuming he hits ahead of Giancarlo Stanton). What may be entirely absent is the power and speed that made him a useful fantasy player in the past. It's been two seasons since he swiped more than three bases, and it wouldn't be surprising if he failed to eclipse 10 home runs in his new home. (Brad Johnson)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Prado-the-Marlin is considerably less exciting than Prado-the-Yankee. 'Tis a shame because his position flexibility is useful.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Hitting fifth in a Miami lineup with Dee Gordon, Christian Yelich, Giancarlo Stanton, and Justin Bour penciled in ahead of him, Prado will have ample opportunities to drive in runs. He may barely crack double digits in home runs, but he’ll give you a .280 average, and if he gets a small boost in batted ball luck, that average will climb accordingly and so will those RBI numbers. He’s going at the back of the pack of third basemen, and justifiably so. If you can stream him against lefties, you can wring some extra benefit out of Prado. He posted good results against them last year, backed by 2.2 walks per strikeout. The fact that he’s hitting fifth for the Marlins says a lot about their lineup, and most of it isn’t very good. Prado hit second or third the majority of the time last year, but that was due to injuries to Stanton and Yelich. That duo hasn’t exactly been the model of health, so don't be surprised to see Prado moving up in the lineup to fill an injury void. He’s making $11 million in 2016 and can be a free agent after the season, so the chances of him ending the season in Marlin teal is the about the same as me learning how to tie a bow-tie. Not an indictment of bow-ties, more an indictment of my tying skills. If he goes somewhere to be a table-setter, he could provide some late-season dividends, but you can’t draft for that. Pick him late, and know he’ll bolster your bench. (Darren Schienbein)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Bears sometimes go to the stream and eat a salmon. If bears played fantasy baseball, they’d stream Prado against lefties. If the bear, no Prado played second base, he’d be an asset, but playing third and the outfield, you can leave him for others. Not to be eaten.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Martin Prado is incredibly versatile and consistent, and that makes him a very valuable real-life player.  The Marlins just extended him for three years and $40 million for that reason even though he is entering his age-33 season.  Typically, fantasy players overrate flawed players who excel in rotisserie fantasy, but Prado is one of the few examples of a player whose real-world value leads players to overvalue him in fantasy.  After six consecutive seasons with between 10 and 15 home runs, Prado has fallen to 9 and 8 home runs the last two seasons.  Meanwhile, he has a combined total of 9 stolen bases in the last four seasons following his outlier season in 2012 with 17 steals.  Prado is as safe as they come in terms of batting average.  He has not hit below .282 in the last five seasons, and his exceptional career 11.0 percent strikeout rate and 90.1 percent contract rate should enable him to maintain that stable average through the back nine of his career.  Still, safe batting average is not enough to propel a player to fantasy relevance outside of NL-only leagues when he provides meager production in the other four categories.  (Scott Spratt)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Prado’s versatility and consistency make him a valuable real-world player, but the same is not true in fantasy.  An excellent and stable batting average does not make up for Prado’s lack of production in the other four categories except in deep and NL-only leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>

Prado hit second or third the majority of the time last year, but that was due to injuries to Stanton and Yelich. That duo hasn’t exactly been the model of health, so don't be surprised to see Prado moving up in the lineup to fill an injury void. He’s making $11 million in 2016 and can be a free agent after the season, so the chances of him ending the season in Marlin teal is the about the same as me learning how to tie a bow-tie. Not an indictment of bow-ties, more an indictment of my tying skills. If he goes somewhere to be a table-setter, he could provide some late-season dividends, but you can’t draft for that.
